1 
1. INTRODUCTION 
This report combines investigation of elastic properties of Paleogene clay performed and presented 
by A. Awadalkarim in his PhD thesis [2], and examination of anisotropic properties of the clay 
from Fehmarn Belt area. In the first part of the report, general description of the project can be 
found which is followed by a brief description of geology from where core samples were collected. 
Next, procedure of laboratory tests is presented together with all parameters and settings used 
during those experiments.  
Furthermore, a work done by A. Awadalkarim [2] is presented. It is focused on the description of 
core samples and their preparation for testing in the form of core plugs. First of all, mineralogy of 
each core sample is presented together with a thorough description of each core plug in a form of 
geotechnical classification.  
The classification includes calculation of bulk density and dry density of each core plug which 
have been based on dimensions and weight of a given sample done before laboratory tests. After 
each test, samples have been measured once again and dried before weighting. This procedure 
helped to calculate all properties of the material (saturation, fluid content, and void ratio) which 
helped to estimate changes taking place within a sample.  
Having introduced samples classification, results of triaxial tests and sonic wave velocity 
measurements done by Awadalkarim [2] are presented. Stress-strain curves for each samples are 
described together with comparison of strain changes for samples from the same core but cut in 
different planes. Next part of this report is devoted to the analysis of the performed triaxial 
experiments. Axial to radial stress relationship is analyzed as this has caused some unpredictable 
errors. Additionally, time of consolidation at each pressure stage of each sample is calculated and 
compared to time used during laboratory tests.  
Triaxial testing analysis is followed by ultrasonic wave velocity measurements description. Plots 
of waveforms from those experiments are presented from which arrival time has been found. This 
helped to calculate P-wave and S-wave velocity. However, the latter one can only be estimated. 
Moreover, quality testing has been performed in order to investigate problems that have occurred 
during triaxial testing. Sample of Paleogene clay have been subjected to the same conditions as in 
experiments performed by Awadalkarim [2]. What is more, a test experiment has also been 
performed on much stiffer chalk material. Results from those two experiments are compared and 
some possible causes and solutions to the problem are presented.  
As a summary to triaxial testing, methods for the calculation of elastic moduli are presented 
together with the obtained results. They have been compared with the ones obtained by 
Awadalkarim [2] and found to be in good agreement. Additionally, comparison between saturated 
elastic modulus and drained elastic modulus has been done.  
==PAGE:7==
2 
As a second part of this report, theoretical background on anisotropy is presented at first. It shows 
all types of anisotropy that the material can exhibit and reasons for its occurrence. Furthermore, 
elastic anisotropy based on L. Thomsen [29] is described together with parameters of weak 
anisotropy (ε, δ, γ) which are crucial for this thesis. In order to fully understand behavior of 
anisotropic material, a literature review of anisotropy in clay and shale has been done. Finally, 
results of Thomsen’s parameter ε calculations are presented and analyzed.  
The calculated results and performed work have been used and presented in a poster [33] at the 
Annual Meeting of Centre for Energy Resources Engineering (CERE) at the Technical University 
of Denmark. It can be found in Appendix 13.7. 
1.1. Project objectives 
The purpose of this thesis is to examine mechanical properties of Paleogene clay from Fehmarn 
Belt area basing on static loading of clay samples as well as sonic wave velocity measurements. 
Data presented in this report hve been obtained from experiments performed by A. Awadalkarim 
[2] which were partly used in his PhD thesis on Petrophysics of Paleogene Clay. The analyzed data 
from laboratory experiments have been used to investigate anisotropic properties of the material. 
Furthermore, mechanical moduli has been calculated both from static (triaxial test) and dynamic 
(velocity measurements) tests. Results from both experiments as well as elastic moduli have been 
used to calculate Thomsen’s parameter ε which defines transverse isotropy.  
1.2. Area of investigation 
Mechanical properties of sedimentary rocks have detrimental influence in petroleum and structural 
engineering. They are influenced by many factors: mineral composition, burial depth, stress 
distribution, temperature, pore pressure, fracturing and consolidation. Knowledge of those 
properties can help to estimate response of a material to drilling or additional loading. The purpose 
of this work is to analyze results of triaxial tests performed on 10 core clay samples from the 
Fehmarn Belt region located between Rødbyhavn in Lolland, Denmark, and Puttgarden in 
Fehmarn, Germany, as presented in Figure 1. Data gathered during tests have been used for the 
calculation of elastic moduli of the material. Furthermore, sonic wave velocity measurements 
performed during different loading and unloading stages helped to find travel time through the core 
samples and estimate influence of material orientation on its physical properties. This study also 
helped to indicate influence of clay compaction on the sonic wave travel time. 
==PAGE:8==
3 
 
Figure 1. Location of Fehmarn Belt region from which clay core samples have been collected. 
1.3. Geology of the studied area 
Samples considered in this study come from four different formations: Røsnæs, Ølst, Æbelø and 
Lillebælt. Røsnæs is considered as both intact and folded deposit, Ølst is identified as intact and 
folded deposit as well, Æbelø is identified as intact formation only and Lillebælt does not have any 
classification in this respect. 
As far as the whole Fehmarn Belt area is concerned, it is composed of Postglacial and Quaternary 
deposits, clays of Paleogene origin, Cretaceous chalk, and clays of Paleogene origin at Lillebælt 
and Fehmarnsund area. Samples analyzed in this study were taken from the Paleogene clay 
formations of various depths. Boreholes used in this study are described in Table 1 and 2. The very 
high plasticity Paleogene clay (grey) presented in figure 2 is overlaid by the Quaternary deposits. 
Those deposits are composed of postglacial and late glacial marine deposits as well as glacial 
deposits (upper and lower clay till, reddish and brown colour). As far as the Paleogene clay is 
concerned, Æbelø and Ølst formations are of Paleocene age whereas, Røsnæs and Lillebælt 
formations are of Eocene age [2].  
As described by Rambøll [22] responsible for geological and geotechnical survey of Fehmarn Belt 
area, water content of all the Paleogene clay formations was in the range of 30 – 45 % which is in 
a good agreement with samples classification presented in Table 5. Furthermore, the average void 
ratio for those formations was found to be 0.87 – 1.20. As far as the plasticity index of clay is 
concerned, it was found to be 80 – 120 % except for Æbelø clay and Lillebælt clay, where values 
==PAGE:9==
4 
of 35 % and 60 % have been found, respectively. A certain difference in the plasticity index of 
Røsnæs intact and folded clay has been found. It is lower by approximately 20 % for intact clay 
than for the folded part however, no explanation has been found in laboratory tests performed by 
Kaufmann [22].   
 
Figure 2. Longitudinal section of the examined area [22]. 
Samples from the considered area are of variable appearance. According to Awadalkarim “The 
Æbelø Formation seems to be silty or even very silty, light grey to grey clay. The Lillebælt Clay 
(which is the youngest Paleogene clay found in the borings at Fehmarn Belt area) tends to be 
greenish to dark greyish green, non to slightly calcareous clay with about 70–80% clay content. 
The Røsnæs Clay is calcareous and silty with some siderite content. Most of it is reddish to 
brownish. However, for the Røsnæs Clay the colour and the general character of the clay change 
significantly down through the layer. The Ølst Clay is non-calcareous and often very dark grey to 
almost black with pyrite concretions.” 
It is important to mention description of the Danish depositional environments by  
Heilmann-Clausen et al. [16]. According to the authors Røsnæs clay is a fine-grained marine clay, 
with a few laminae of black clay in the uppermost bed. Whereas, the Ølst clay is a coarser-grained 
material with grains reaching almost one centimeter in diameter for some cases. As far as the 
Lillebælt formation is concerned, it is extremely fine-grained with slightly silty beds near the base 
and top of the formation.  
1.4. Triaxial strain experiments and sonic wave velocity 
Consolidation experiments were performed on 10 sets of Paleogene clay samples which were 
extracted from full core samples taken from the field. Each set of Paleogene clay contained 3 core 
plugs cut in horizontal, vertical and tangential (45ᴼ) direction. Samples were naturally saturated 
with water and their petrophysical properties will be presented in table 3 and 4. Each core plug had 
the dimensions close to 37.5 mm in height and 25 mm in diameter. Those dimensions may vary 
slightly between samples which is caused by softness of the material and direction of cutting.  
==PAGE:10==
5 
As far as the triaxial strain experiment is concerned, it has been performed under drained conditions 
in a GDS load frame. Sample has been placed in a Hoek cell, supported with loading pistons in 
vertical direction and the confining pressure has been increased to 0.2 MPa. This helped to transfer 
Hoek cell, with sample inside, onto a load frame in a safely manner. As this has been achieved, 
sample has been loaded vertically with the pressure of 0.2 MPa to ensure stability.  
As the last step, three strain gauges were mounted together with pulse generator and receiver which 
were connected to the oscilloscope. Strain gauges were measuring sample deformation in vertical 
axis only. The whole set of the experimental setup is presented in Figure 3. 
Tests were performed in pressure range of 0.2 – 3 MPa for axial load whereas, the confining 
pressure was adjusted in way so that the ratio of radial stress and axial stress was equal to 0.55. 
This value has been determined basing on the coefficient of earth’s pressure at rest (K0) as shown 
in Equation 20 [24]: 
𝜎𝑟
𝜎𝑎
= 𝐾0
𝑂𝐶= (𝐾0
𝑁𝐶) ∙(𝑂𝐶𝑅0.55) 
(1) 
Where K0 is the coefficient of earth’s pressure at rest, 𝐾0
𝑁𝐶 is the normally consolidated K0 which 
is ≈0.55, OCR is the over-consolidation ratio which is the ratio between the pre-consolidation 
stress (𝜎𝑝𝑐
′ ) and current in-situ vertical effective stress (𝜎𝑉0
′ ) and it is assumed to be equal to 1 in 
uploading. 
 
Figure 3. Schematic drawing of the experimental setup for triaxial strain and elastic wave 
velocity measurements (Awadalkarim [2]). 
As far as measuring of sound wave velocity is concerned, it has been done simultaneously with the 
stress-strain tests in 4 consecutive stages: 0.6 MPa, 1 MPa, 2 MPa, and 3 MPa for uploading and  
2 MPa, 1 MPa, and 0.6 MPa for unloading. Every sample was kept on each stage for a time which 
would ensure drained condition of a sample close to 100 % drainage. This means that there is no 
influence of pore water on the compaction of a sample and the lateral strain is considered as being 
equal to zero. Unfortunately some errors have been encountered in this regard which are described 
in section concerning results of experiments.  
==PAGE:11==
6 
Sound wave velocity was measured both for pressure wave (P-wave) and for shear wave (S-wave). 
For this purpose wave frequency of 200 kHz has been generated by means of pulse transmission 
technique. “The system delay times were estimated by linear regression of travel time versus 
distance measured on the three standard steel plugs of different lengths. The system delay times 
are 3 and 7 μsec for the P-wave and S-wave velocities, respectively. [2]” 
As far as estimation of the arrival time is concerned, the first arrival of the waveform indicated 
arrival time for P-wave and the first zero-crossing of the waveform indicates arrival time for the  
S-wave. The P-wave arrival times were relatively easy to notice which was not the case for the 
shear waves. S-wave was usually interfered by P-wave and probably due to misalignment of 
loading pistons with the sample (small gaps, clay crystals reflecting shear waves) it was very 
difficult or even impossible to read accurate arrival time. In order to achieve final velocity of travel 
through a sample, its initial length has been used as follows: 
𝑉=
𝐿
𝑡𝑎𝑟𝑟𝑖𝑣𝑎𝑙−𝑡𝑑𝑒𝑙𝑎𝑦
 
(2) 
Where (tarrival – tdelay) is the interval needed for the elastic wave to cross through the sample and L 
is the actual sample length corrected for uniaxial deformation.  
==PAGE:12==

2. RESULTS OF WORK PERFORMED BY  
A. AWADALKARIM [2] 
Samples used in this study, have been collected from Fehmarn Belt area as it has been described in 
the introduction. Sediment samples have been obtained from 24 boreholes distributed in the 
examined area. However, for the sake of this thesis, results from tests on samples from only 10 
boreholes have been used. Plug samples in three directions have been cut out from core samples 
(Fig. 4) which gives a total number of 30 samples. Depth of core samples was different, depending 
on the borehole, ranging from 2.8 m up to 94 m below sea level. The major part of examined 
samples were of Paleogene clay origin. It is the most challenging material with possible  
micro-cracks and minor fractures as well as high plasticity, which makes it difficult to cut and 
prepare core plugs for experiments.  
Basing on XRD analysis and BET measurement results presented by Awadalkarim [2], samples 
come from five different formations: Røsnæs, Ølst, Claytill, Æbelø and Lillebælt however, samples 
from Claytill have not been used in this thesis. As far as XRD analysis of samples is concerned, 
the estimated mineralogy of 30 analyzed samples is presented in Table 1.  
As it can be seen from Table 1, smectite and illite exist in all samples and they account for main 
constituents in the analyzed samples. Occurrence of smectite is especially important as it has 
substantial influence on anisotropy of the material. In most of the cases content of smectite is higher 
than 50 % of all clay minerals with similar or lower amount of illite. As far as non-clay minerals 
are concerned, quartz and feldspar were found in all examined samples. In all cases, amount of 
quartz is rather high exceeding 20 % except from samples 93 and 95. Whereas, content of feldspar 
is usually below 5 % except from sample 93. Only sample 95 has exceptionally high content of 
calcite (66 %) and according to Awadalkarim [2], the sample may not be fully representative. 
As far as BET surface area is concerned, it exceeds 60 m2/g for all samples and is the highest for 
samples 31 and 93 which come from Ølst formation. Furthermore, in order to classify the analyzed 
samples and fully understand their properties some basic geotechnical classification has been done. 
After triaxial testing each sample was measured and placed in oven for drying for 3 days (60 ᴼC) 
which was followed by weighting of the sample and placing in oven for another drying for 3 days 
(105 ᴼC). The second drying stage was performed in order to verify water content of samples – 
whether there is any change for higher temperatures. The gathered data, together with initial (before 
triaxial testing) dimensions were used to find water content, porosity, saturation, bulk and dry 
density, as well as change in void ratio, strain and water content. Initial dimensions and weight of 
samples as well as dimensions after compression tests and weight after drying stages are presented 
in Table 3. Whereas, results of further calculations are presented in Table 4.

As it can be seen from Table 4, the studied clay samples are not fully saturated which may be 
caused by the loss of water content during transport of core samples, preparation of core plug and 
their handling, weighting and placing the sample in the triaxial Hoek cell for the consolidation test. 
Core cylinders preparation seems to be crucial parameter for the water saturation deviations as the 
material is very plastic. It was especially challenging for samples cut in vertical plane. 
As far as formulas used for calculations of all values presented in Table 3 and 4 are concerned, 
initial bulk density has been calculated basing on values of initial weight and volume which are 
presented in Table 3. The same applies to final bulk density which is based on sample’s weight and 
volume after the consolidation test:

3. EVALUATION OF STRESS-STRAIN RELATIONSHIP 
As it has been mentioned before, data presented in this dissertation have been obtained from 
experiments performed by Awadalkarim [2]. In his thesis only the vertical samples are presented 
whereas, this section contains results from horizontal, vertical, and tangential data. The chapter is 
divided into two subsections: stress-strain compaction tests and sonic wave velocity tests. For the 
sake of this report only some typical results are presented in this section whereas, data for the rest 
of samples can be found in Appendix 13.5.  
3.1. Stress-strain relationship 
As it has been explained in the experiment’s procedure, samples have been subjected to linearly 
increasing axial and radial load. The maximum applied stress was equal to 3 MPa and results of 
the experiment for all horizontal vertical and tangential samples are presented in Figures 4, 5, and 
6 respectively. As it can be seen from Figure 4, there are very different strain results between 
samples. Sample 89, 1, 93, and 2 have been deflected by more than 20 % whereas, deformation for 
sample 89 is the highest out of all and is equal to 30 %. As far as the rest of the samples are 
concerned, they have been deformed by less than 15 % (sample 31) with the lowest value for 
sample 95 with strain equal to 3.2%. This could be explained by extremely high calcite content 
which has been mentioned in previous section. Furthermore, there must have been a problem with 
loading frame or strain gauges. As it can be seen from stress-strain plot for sample 8, there has 
been a sudden increase in deformation during unloading stage. Probably the error is related to 
software or signal processing due to the fact that the change in strain is sudden – it happens within 
one iteration and it comes back to the expected value in the same manner. Similar problems have 
occurred for vertical and tangential samples which is presented in Figure 5 and 6. 
As far as vertical samples are concerned, sample 29 has reached the highest deformation equal to 
23 % and sample 95 with deformation of over 16 %. Whereas, the rest of the samples did not reach 
more than 15 % with the lowest value of 7.2 % for sample number 2. Similarly to errors with 
sudden strain jumps between axial loading stages, the same problem occurred for vertical sample 
93. There is a decrease in strain between 1 MPa and 2 MPa uploading stages which has to be 
corrected. 
When results for tangential samples are analyzed, it can be seen that there have also been some 
equipment related errors for sample 37 however, they did not influence measurements in any 
significant way. Furthermore, similarly to horizontal and vertical samples, stress-strain experiment 
results are very versatile. The highest deformation equal to 18 % has been measured for sample 31 
and 29. Whereas, samples 1 and 2 has undergone the smallest deformation which equaled 6.5 %. 
Unfortunately the experiments performed by Awadalkarim [2] did not include results for horizontal 
and vertical core plugs for sample 15 therefore, it has been omitted in any further analysis.

As it has been mentioned before, samples examined in this report have been extracted from 6 
different formations: Røsnæs intact (sample 8, 15, and 29), Røsnæs folded (sample 37), Ølst intact 
(sample 93), Ølst folded (sample 31), Æbelø intact (sample 1 and 2) and Lillebælt (sample 89 and 
95). Results from stress – strain experiment for each sample have been presented in figures 7 to 11. 
It presents a comparison of core plugs cut in different planes for each sample and for all uploading 
stages: 0.6 MPa, 1 MPa, 2 MPa, and 3 MPa. There is a visible anisotropy in the response of core 
plugs to the applied pressure. It is especially noticable for sample 29, 93, 1, and 95. Clay response 
in all stages of those samples is different than in the other two cutting planes. This shows that 
material is anisotropic even for very low axial load which is much lower than the effective stress 
found in the field. Furthermore, for most of the samples, the highest strain occurred in vertical 
direction. This can be explained by the fact that clay has been deposited in layers oriented in 
horizontal direction and gravity induced compaction caused higher rigidity of the material in this 
respect. Hence, those samples are usually more prone to compaction and failure in vertical direction 
due to the fact that force is applied parallelly to the vertical stress. It will encounter much higher 
resistance if it is applied in a perpendicular direction to the deposited layers. This statement is 
especially true when confining pressure is applied which restricts sample from expansion in the 
direction perpendicular to the applied force (e.g. confining pressure).  
Røsnæs intact

3.2. Axial – radial stress relationship 
During analysis of data gathered from experiments performed by Awadalkarim [2] a problem with 
both uploading and unloading stages have been found. Normally, both of those stresses should 
increase/decrease linearly as it has been commanded by the machinery operator. An exemplary plot 
of axial versus radial load of sample 8 has been presented in Figure 12. As it can be seen, confining 
pressure increases/decreases in a proper manner with slightly different rate than the axial load 
however, due to the initial condition of ratio between radial to axial load (0.55), it is the proper way 
of performing the experiment.  
 
Figure 12. Axial stress vs radial stress for horizontal sample 8. 
Whereas, Figure 13 presents some typical plots of axial versus radial load for other samples. The 
rest of the plots can be found in Appendix 13.2. As it can be seen, load paths are totally different 
than the one presented in Figure 12. 
As long as the plot for vertical sample 37 looks slightly better than for the rest of samples, it is still 
far from satisfactory. Furthermore, it is just one sample presenting such stress paths whereas, 90 % 
of samples present results as the ones in Figure 13. The main problem is the fact that at low stress 
values below 1 MPa, both axial and radial stresses increase/decrease properly but for the rest of the 
experiment there are very rapid changes in the axial load. When 1 MPa is reached, confining 
pressure increases slowly to the next level just as it has been commanded. However, there is no 
change or very small change in axial load for the whole uploading/unloading stage. Until the last 
1 or 2 iterations when it rapidly jumps to the next level e.g. from 1 MPa to 2 MPa. It is an 
unexpected problem which we did not have knowledge about, before analyzing the data.  
The presented problem may have a huge influence on the final result of the experiment due to the 
fact that a sample do not have enough time during each uploading stage to expel pore water. 
Therefore, although the experiment is performed in drained conditions it cannot be treated as such. 
A solution to the problem could be, to keep a sample at a given stage (e.g. at 1 MPa, 2 MPa, and  
3 MPa) for a reasonably long time however, when a closer look is taken to the data it has not been 
achieved.

==PAGE:25==

Figure 13. Axial load vs radial load for 4 typical samples. 
Figure 14 shows stress-strain plots with the indication of time under consolidation at each 
uploading and unloading stage for sample 31. Plots for the rest of samples can be found in 
Appendix 13.6. As it can be seen in Figure 14, time of sample’s consolidation at each stage varies 
a lot, e.g. for vertical core plug time under the load of 3 MPa is only 50 minutes whereas, in other 
cases it reaches more than 800 minutes. The long time indicates that some of the stages were left 
overnight but the very short time is what worries the most. 50 or 60 minutes is not enough for the 
sample to reach full consolidation for a given stage, as a result there is an influence of pore water 
pressure on the deformation of a sample. In order to check what time is required for full 
consolidation at a given stage, equation 13 has been used.

The purpose of making time curve for deformation is to find when a sample has reached full 
primary consolidation. It is done by plotting two lines tangent to the deformation curve as shown

each sample is vital in order to define whether they have undergone a full primary consolidation.

(Msat) and dry (Mdry) moduli. However, due to the fact that it cannot be decided whether or not all 
samples have undergone a full consolidation, Mdrain has been calculated as secant to last points in 
stress – strain stages which will be explained in section 3.5 (Figure 22).

3.4. Quality testing 
The measuring errors which occurred during the analysis of results from uniaxial stress-strain 
experiments were unexpected and their origin is unknown. It can only be speculated that those 
problems are due to samples high plasticity or mechanical failure of the testing stand to properly 
analyse signal from sample that is being compressed. Therefore, it has been decided to perform a 
quality testing experiment on naturally saturated sample from one of the core samples used by 
Awadalkarim [2]. The test has been performed in the same conditions as the ones done by 
Awadalkarim with one exception: after each stage has been reached, the sample was kept in the 
assigned pressure for 4 hours in order to assure full compaction which has been calculated from 
Equation 22. Results of the test are presented in Figure 20. 
 
Figure 20. Stress-strain relation for test sample. 
As it can be seen from Figure 20, the plotted stress – strain relation is very similar to those presented 
in Figure 9, 10, and 11. Unfortunately the same error has been encountered: as the axial load has 
reached 1 MPa, the increase/decrease in pressure occurred at the very last moment of each stage 
for pressure higher than 1 MPa. As the experiment has been thoroughly observed at all times, it has 
been noticed that as the 1 MPa stage has been passed, there was only an increase in radial stress 
which has been marked in Figure 20 with red circles. Those intervals show that there is almost no 
change in sample’s length however, there is a visible increase in axial load although there was no 
action taken by the step motor controlling the axial load. This means that increase in confining 
pressure only caused the sample to expand in axial direction putting additional stress on the loading 
pistons. As a result the software might have analysed that the axial load has already been increased 
and commanded the step motor to stay in the same position. This could be one of many explanations 
to the problem however, it is still unknown why this did not happen to pressure lower than 1 MPa.  
0
0,2
0,4
0,6
0,8
1
1,2
1,4
1,6

0,5

Test sample
==PAGE:35==
30 
In order to verify if the error was caused by high plasticity of the examined clay, a much stiffer 
material (chalk) has also been subjected to the same experimental procedure. Results of the 
performed experiment are presented in Figure 21. As it can be seen, the stress – strain curve 
presents a much more satisfactory results when compared to the ones obtained for clay. There is a 
steady increase/decrease of both axial and confining pressure throughout the whole experiment. 
Due to the fact that chalk is a much harder material and in such pressure range (maximum of  
3 MPa) it undergoes very low axial deformation. Hence, highly plastic properties of clay material 
could be the reason for faulty conduction of the experiment.  
 
Figure 21. Stress-strain plot for chalk sample used to check influence of typed commands on the 
behaviour of the load frame's step motor. 
Another reason for faulty behaviour of the experimental stand might be commands sent to the 
equipment by operating software. From original data files it has been found that commands sent to 
the axial load frame and pressure chamber controlling pressure in the Hoek cell requested 
independent increase/decrease of axial load and confining pressure. Therefore, theoretically the 
software could have not increased axial load if the confining pressure has been increased. Maybe 
if the ratio of 0.55 for axial and radial load had been set as the command, this type of error might 
have not occurred. Unfortunately, due to the lack of time, we could not perform another experiment 
with changed commands.

3.5. Elastic moduli of clay 
Elastic properties of a material can be defined by its elastic moduli. Those are dependent on the 
state of a rock’s sample e.g. saturation, stress state, consolidation, mineralogy. As it has been 
mentioned before, drained modulus (Mdrain), undrained modulus (Msat) and dry modulus (Mdry) has 
been calculated basing on drained triaxial tests and sonic wave measurements performed during 
the experiment. As far as Mdrain is concerned it is a ratio between axial pressure change and the 
change in strain of the sample: 
𝑀𝑑𝑟𝑎𝑖𝑛= ∆𝜎
∆𝜀 
(17) 
Where ∆𝜎 is the vertical pressure change and ∆𝜀 is the vertical change in strain. As it has been 
presented in Figure 15 it cannot be stated that samples have undergone a full consolidation. Very 
rapid change in axial load in the end of each stage might have caused exceptionally high creep of 
samples. Therefore, wherever it was applicable, calculation of Mdrain has been based on point taken 
from time curves as shown in Figure 22. It has been done only for those pressure stages where the 
consolidation time was long enough to find lines secant to the time curve (Figure 17). Otherwise, 
a slope of a line tangent to the endpoints of each pressure stage has been used as presented in Figure 
22. Although there is an influence of creep on the Mdrain due to the very rapid consolidation in the 
end of uploading stage, it is the only reasonable solution for this set of data. 
 
Figure 22. Lines tangent to the stress-strain curve stages used for calculation of drained 
modulus.

As far as the undrained modulus is concerned, it is a P-wave modulus calculated from the velocity 
of pressure wave crossing the sample: 
𝑀𝑠𝑎𝑡= 𝜌𝑏𝑣𝑃
2 
(18) 
Where 𝜌𝑏 is the bulk density [g/cm3] of a sample calculated according to equation 3 and 𝑣𝑃 is the 
velocity [m/s] of P-wave passing the sample. Calculations of Msat are based on velocity 
measurements which have been performed at the end of each pressure stage.  
3.5.1. Dry modulus calculation from Gassmann equation 
Dry modulus of clay samples has been calculated in this study by means of Gassmann’s equation 
(Gassman, 1951 [13]). The main assumption of Gassman derivation is that the whole framework 
of a material is homogeneous and the same at different fluid saturations. Furthermore, a uniformly 
distributed induced pressure is assumed and there is no influence of saturation on undrained moduli 
of a material. Gassmann fluid substitution is a very convenient model as it can be adjusted to a 
specific formation (Sørensen [28]).  
As far as Gassmann’s equation is concerned, it is a relation between the saturated bulk modulus of 
a material and its porosity, bulk modulus of the porous rock frame, the bulk modulus of the mineral 
matrix and the bulk modulus of the fluid inside the pore space: 
𝑀𝑠𝑎𝑡= 𝑀𝑑𝑟𝑦+
(1 −𝑀𝑑𝑟𝑦
𝑀𝑔𝑟𝑎𝑖𝑛)
2
∅
𝑀𝑓𝑙𝑢𝑖𝑑+ 1 −∅
𝑀𝑔𝑟𝑎𝑖𝑛−𝑀𝑑𝑟𝑦
𝑀𝑔𝑟𝑎𝑖𝑛
2
 
 
(19) 
Where Msat is the saturated bulk modulus, Mgrain is the bulk modulus of mineral matrix, Mfluid is the 
bulk modulus of the pore fluid, Mdry is the bulk modulus of the porous rock frame (with no pore 
fluid inside) and ø is the porosity. When considering the application of the rearranged Gassmann 
equation the previously mentioned assumptions have to be considered. Moreover, basing on the 
article presented by Al-Khateb [1] it has to be assumed that: 
 the examined porous material is elastic and composed of one type of minerals 
 there is no cementation or dissolution of the pores – porosity remains the same throughout 
the experiment 
 as mentioned before, there is an equal pressure distribution within the porous space – 
pressure equilibrium in the whole sample is assumed 
 there is no fluid-movement in or out of the sample (undrained conditions) 
 there are no chemical interactions of any chemicals with the rock frame – shear modulus of 
the rock remains constant 
The formula in Equation 11 is rearranged and used for the calculation of Mdry. As the porosity of a 
given sample together with Mfluid and M0 is known whereas, Msat is obtained from sonic wave 
velocity measurements, Mdry can be calculated as follows: 
==PAGE:38==
33 
𝑀𝑑𝑟𝑦=
𝑀𝑠𝑎𝑡(∅𝑀𝑔𝑟𝑎𝑖𝑛
𝑀𝑓𝑙
+ 1 −∅) −𝑀𝑔𝑟𝑎𝑖𝑛
∅𝑀𝑔𝑟𝑎𝑖𝑛
𝑀𝑓𝑙
+ 𝑀𝑠𝑎𝑡
𝑀𝑔𝑟𝑎𝑖𝑛−1 −∅
 
(20) 
It has been assumed that the lower bound of Mgrain is equal to Young’s modulus of grains E which 
can be estimated from grain density (ρg) as described by Awadalkarim [2]: 
𝐸= −377 + 0.189𝜌𝑔 
(21) 
Unfortunately the S-wave modulus, G, could not be calculated due to the highly inaccurate results 
of the S-wave arrival times. Calculation results for Mdrain, Msat, and Mdry moduli are presented in 
Figure 23, 24 and 25.

As it can be seen from the presented plots, the saturated modulus, Msat is always the highest. It is 
due to the fact that P-wave velocity used in the calculation of the modulus is influenced by pore 
fluid existing in samples. Therefore, velocity of P-wave is increased in the vicinity of water which 
in result, increases undrained (saturated) modulus. As the axial load on a sample is increased, water 
is squeezed out of the sample decreasing void space which increases contact area between grains 
of a sample. As a result, Msat is usually increased for the increasing pressure.  
As far as the drained modulus, Mdrain, is concerned, it is obtained from static mechanical loading 
of a sample. Although it is called a “drained” modulus, a 100 % drainage can never be obtained 
hence, there is always an influence of pore fluid on the stiffness of a sample. Moreover, as it has 
been described earlier, static loading was performed in a very short period of time which caused 
that even more fluid might have remained in a sample causing increase in the drained modulus. 
Furthermore, the very short consolidation time also caused additional plastic deformation of a 
sample, increasing strain and thus, decreasing Mdrain.  
Finally, the dry modulus, Mdry, is a representation of the solid frame of a sample only. Therefore, 
there is no influence of pore fluid on the calculations. It is always slightly higher than Mdrain and 
the difference is increased with the increasing pressure. It is caused due to the fact that the 
Gassmann’s equation is based on the Msat and porosity of a sample. With the increasing undrained 
modulus and decrease in porosity, Mdry will increase as well. The difference between those two 
moduli (Mdrain and Mdry) is probably caused by fluid existing in sample which influences the Mdrain 
calculations. 
As far as anisotropy of elastic moduli is concerned, comparison of each sample is presented in 
Figure 26. For every sample there is a visible difference in drained modulus whereas, differences 
of the saturated modulus are much lower between samples cut in different planes. No significant 
trend can be found for all sets of core samples however a difference between moduli can be seen. 
For further investigation of anisotropy, Thomsen’s parameter ε has to be investigated which is 
described in the next section.

4. ANISOTROPY OF THE STUDIED SAMPLES 
In order to begin the analysis of anisotropic properties of Paleogene clay from Fehmarn Belt area, 
a theoretical introduction, of what anisotropy is, has to be established. Some short description of 
different types of anisotropy will be presented in this section together with examples from the 
literature. 
4.1. Theoretical background 
Anisotropic media poses variations in physical properties in different direction of the space which, 
for geological media, is often caused by sedimentation, tectonics, etc. This means that the elastic 
response of a material is not independent of the material’s orientation for a given stress 
configuration as it has been stated by Fjær et al. [11]. This is especially true for elastic properties 
of clay which is investigated in this study. This material is a constituent to most sedimentary rocks 
such as shale and mudstones which account for 60 – 70 % of sedimentary basins (Bandyopadhyay 
[4]). Most of clay materials behave in an anisotropic way which is mainly caused by the rate of 
their deposition. This accounts for layers of periodic deposition of finer and coarser particle sizes. 
Moreover, it is common for clay to be varved, laminated or deposited in a layered fashion. 
Preferred orientation of particles is also an anisotropic factor influencing prefailure stress-strain 
relations and the strength of the material which, according to Kirkgard et al. [23], tend to be 
different with direction [23]. Influence of depositional environment and pre-consolidation history 
has been studied before by Gilligan and Clemence 1984 [14], and Quigley and Thompson 1966. 
According to Quigley and Thompson [26] the structure of soils can be regarded as consisting of 
two components: 
 “Fabric, which is the geometrical arrangement of the particles 
 The nature and strength of the bonds acting between these particles” 
The experiment performed by the Quigley and Thomson [26] utilized Leda clay from Ottawa 
region which was composed of clay size materials (86 %) and silt size materials (14 %). It 
contained clay minerals such as: illite and chlorite, interlayered illite-chlorite and vermiculite as 
well as a small amount of montomorillonite-like swelling mineral. Naturally, quartz and feldspar 
were present in the sample. It has been found that anisotropic consolidation (which may be 
different in one or more directions) of undisturbed samples is causing changes in the orientation 
of clay “particles into a plane which is perpendicular to the direction of the major principal 
consolidation pressure” [26]. As a result properties of the material become anisotropic.  
Except from anisotropy induced by stress applied to the naturally occurring material, it can also 
be caused by:  
 Crystals orientation which is caused by the direction of deposition 
 Lithology 
 Fractures, cracks, and pores within the material 
==PAGE:45==
40 
Rocks anisotropy can be divided into two different groups. One is dependent on the direction of 
anisotropy whereas, the other one is strictly related to the source of anisotropy. As far as direction 
dependent anisotropy is concerned, there are two types of it and both are called transverse isotropy. 
They differ in the axis of symmetry where material possesses the same properties in two directions 
and different ones in the third. On the other hand, source related anisotropy can be either intrinsic 
or induced. Intrinsic anisotropy is caused by small scale heterogeneity caused by the occurrence 
of sequences of different rock types. Whereas, the induced anisotropy is caused by the strain 
engendered by the applied stress, fractures and diagenesis [11]. 
4.1.1. Transverse isotropy 
As it has been mentioned before, there are two types of transverse anisotropy. They differ only in 
the axis of symmetry – this means that if the material is rotated around that axis, its properties 
should remain unchanged. This kind of anisotropy, is considered to be representative symmetry 
for horizontally layered sedimentary rocks. Stress induced anisotropy may often be described by 
transverse isotropy as well [11]. Hence, elastic properties of the material are equal in all directions 
within a plane. As a result, two types of transverse anisotropy can be distinguished:  
 VTI – vertical transverse isotropy is found where gravity is the dominant factor. It is related 
to thin layering of sedimentary rocks with vertical axis of symmetry. Therefore, elastic 
properties of the material will vary vertically between each layer, but not horizontally. 
 HTI – horizontal transverse isotropy is often related to vertical cracks and fractures acting 
as a weak points of the material. What is more, unequal horizontal stresses also alter the 
material which may become transversely isotropic. As a result of fractures, elastic 
properties of sedimentary rocks vary in the direction crossing the fractures but remain the 
same along the plane of fractures.  
 
Figure 27. Illustration of a) VTI and b) HTI (Backus [3]). 
There have been many articles concerning transverse anisotropy which were based on 
experimental data. G.E. Backus [3] described the influence of horizontal layering on elastic 
anisotropy and J.G. Berryman [7] focused on elastic anisotropy in transversely isotropic media. 
However, article by L. Thomsen [29] is one of the most cited ones and focuses on weak elastic 
anisotropy. The author has proposed a set of equations governing weak anisotropy and has 
indicated that most bulk elastic media are weakly anisotropic. Thus, parameters presented in the 
==PAGE:46==
41 
article are applicable to most of the materials met in geophysical exploration. Thomsen’s 
parameters and weak anisotropy will be discussed later in this thesis.  
4.1.2. Intrinsic anisotropy 
As sedimentary rocks are created during a deposition process, they are usually not oriented 
randomly. In the case of clastic sediments, anisotropy effects may arise during and after deposition. 
For instance, river deposits, streams, tidal deposits, beaches and shelves, deep sea deposits are a 
good example of intrinsic anisotropy as stated by Ruiz [27].  
As the water deposits rock particles in its path, it is done in a preferred direction of the river flow. 
Hence, plane grains will be oriented in the direction of the stream. Furthermore, anisotropy may 
also be induced by seasonal variations in the fluid flow resulting in alternating layers of fine and 
coarse grains. As a result, elastic moduli of rocks created by this exemplary river flow will be 
dependent on the orientation of the material [11]. 
4.1.3. Induced anisotropy 
This type of anisotropy is caused by micro-cracks resulting from external stress. They are usually 
oriented normally to the lowest principal stress. The anisotropy is induced after depositions due to 
physical, chemical, and biological alterations of sediment material. What is more, compaction and 
overburden stress may cause rotation of grain axes into the horizontal plane. Induced anisotropy 
may also be caused by dewatering of clay material as well as dissolution of clay grains at their 
contact point. An example of intrinsic and induced anisotropy is presented in Figure 28 [11].  
 
Figure 28. Intrinsic (left) and stress induced (right) anisotropy [11]. 
4.2. Elastic anisotropy 
In order to define elastic anisotropy, it is good to define mathematical definition of elastic material 
first. If a material possesses linear anisotropy, each stress component σij is dependent upon every 
component of strain εkl. When a material is considered to be under stress in all three directions  
(x, y, and z) then directional indexes i, j, k, l may have values of 1, 2, and 3. Therefore, there are 
nine relations between stress and strain, each involving one component of stress and nine 
components of strain which can be represented as follows: 
==PAGE:47==
42 
𝜎𝑖𝑗= ∑∑𝐶𝑖𝑗𝑘𝑙𝜀𝑘𝑙,   𝑖, 𝑗= 1,2,3
3

the medium. Because of the symmetry of the material, stress components are as follows:

(23) 
 
Therefore, only six of those relations are independent. The same situation applies to strain:

(24) 
 
Hence, only six terms from the relation (22) are independent. As a result, the elasticity may be 
represented by Voigt notation as follows:

(25)

L. Thomsen has used phase velocities (30) and replaced five elastic moduli used in the equation

they are: 
 nondimensional, which gives rational expression of anisotropy in percent, 
 a proper combination of elastic moduli to simplify Equation 30 (a, b, c),

4.3. Literature review 
It is important to state that shale as a material is composed of clay materials and it is a very common 
rock met in petroleum engineering. Therefore, a brief description of shale is included in here as 
well. Anisotropy of both clay and shale can be influenced by such factors as: stress state and stress 
history, smectite content, organic content, microstructure and physiochemical interactions with 
pore fluids. A short literature examples are presented in this paragraph which show influence of 
those factors on elastic behavior of both clay and shale. As the Paleogene clay is rich in smectite, 
a separate subsection has been devoted to this mineral.  
4.3.1. Anisotropy of clay 
As it has been mentioned before, elastic anisotropy is controlled by the intrinsic anisotropy and by 
textural alignment of clay grains, pores, and fractures. As L. Thomsen [29] has stated, most bulk 
elastic media are weakly anisotropic however, “in most applications of elasticity theory to 
problems in petroleum geophysics, the elastic medium is assumed to be isotropic”. It is due to the 
fact that weak anisotropy accounts for differences in elastic properties of a rock which are below 
10 %. Thus, for simplification, materials are treated as isotropic media. This neglect may lead to 
incorrect estimates of rock and fluid properties.  
Furthermore, prediction of elastic anisotropy of clay or any other rock is difficult if the elastic 
properties of rock-forming clay minerals are not known. K. Bandyopadhyay et al. [5] have 
investigated compressional and shear wave anisotropy of montmorillonitic clays at variable 
porosities and crystalline alignment. Similarly to L. Thomsen, the authors have stated that many 
results presenting elastic properties of clays and shales in scientific papers assume isotropy of the 
considered medium which is wrong. This might be the reason for versatile reported values for clay 
moduli. Katahara [21] reports bulk modulus K≈50 GPa and shear modulus μ≈20 GPa. Whereas, 
results from laboratory and well log measurements in shales and laminated shaley sands, 
performed by Bandyopadhyay et al. [5], show much lower values of 20 GPa and 7 GPa for K and 
μ respectively [5]. Vanorio et al. [30] reports values of the solid clay phase in the range of 6 – 12 
GPa and 4 – 6 GPa for bulk and shear modulus respectively. Therefore, the literature results show 
a huge difference in bulk and shear moduli values. Through laboratory experiments, 
Bandyopadhyay et al. have proven high anisotropy of montmorillonitic clays by measuring 
porosity, grain density, bulk density, vp and vs which were used to calculate Thomsen’s anisotropy 
parameters. ε and γ. Results of those calculations are presented in Figure 30. 
==PAGE:51==
46 
 
Figure 30. Thomsen's parameters ε and γ for montmorillonitic clays, smectite and kaolinite [5]. 
As it can be seen in Figure 30, montmorillonitic clay may reach values for Thomsen’s parameters 
as high as 0.35 for ε and 0.12 for γ. Values for smectite are especially high.  
Similarly to Bandyopadhyay et al., Jones et al. [20] have performed measurement of elastic 
properties of solid clays (clays without pores). Significance of this experiment is especially high 
because clay minerals are the most abundant ones in the earth’s crust. However, it is very hard to 
find large enough single crystals of clay minerals which is a common procedure for measuring 
elastic properties of a mineral. Hence, the authors have mixed clay minerals with a material of a 
known elastic properties and inverted the obtained results by means of Hashin-Shtrikman average. 
For the purpose if this experiment, 16 types of clay s have been used which were mixed with quartz 
sands of various grain size distributions, pure calcium carbonate (CaCO3) powder, limestone 
powder, dolomite powder of different grain sizes, sodium chloride (NaCl) powder and crystals, 
etc. Results of the analyzed samples are presented in Table 8. As the authors state, “although errors 
exist in the inverted data (less than 10 %), the elastic properties of solid clays […] are by far the 
most accurate and comprehensive data” (Jones et al. [20]).  
As far as anisotropy of clay material is concerned, Hoque and Islam [18] have investigated strength 
anisotropy of Dhaka clay. It is an over-consolidated clay formed by older Pleistocene sediments. 
The material was grinded mechanically, passed through a sieve and mixed with water. The 
obtained mixture was poured into a mold layer by layer. The molded material was used to cut three 
test specimens in horizontal, vertical and tangential direction which were subjected to compaction. 
It has been found that strength of the compacted clay was directionally dependent. The coefficient 
of anisotropy (strength of vertical specimen/strength of horizontal specimen) was varied between 
0.75 – 1.25 in unconfined compression tests.  
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:52==
47 
Table 8. Elastic properties of clays measured by Jones et al. [20]. 
 
Mondol [25] has investigated velocity anisotropy in experimentally compacted clay-silt and  
clay-clay mixtures with varying textural and mineralogical compositions. Similarly to laboratory 
procedure performed by Awadalkarim [2], N. H. Mondol has measured P-wave and S-wave 
velocities simultaneously with the increasing stress levels. Experiments were done as uniaxial 
strain tests in one of Norwegian Geotechnical Institute’s triaxial cells. Samples for the tests were 
prepared from characterized synthetic mudstones of known amounts of clay-silt and clay-clay. The 
prepared samples were precompacted to 3 MPa and velocity measurements were taken at effective 
stresses 5 MPa, 10 MPa, 20 MPa, 30 MPa and 50 MPa. As expected, velocities in vertical and 
horizontal directions increase with the increase in effective stress (Figure 31). Furthermore, it has 
been found that horizontal velocities (both for pressure and shear waves) are generally higher when 
compared to velocities from vertical samples.  
==PAGE:53==
48 
 
Figure 31. Dependence of vp and vS on the applied stress for vertical samples. 
 As far as Thomsen’s parameters, for the examined samples, are concerned, ε varies from 0 to 0.15 
whereas, γ gives much higher values ranging from 0 up to 0.62. Mondol [25] has concluded that 
maximum velocity anisotropy occurs for samples dominated by kaolinite. 
4.3.2. Anisotropy of shale 
Few data exist on the anisotropy and elastic properties of clays, as they are not as significant as 
shale or sandstone in the oil and gas exploration which are either hydrocarbon-carrying media or 
flow barriers. What is more, about 75 % of all sedimentary rocks is composed of shale. 
Additionally, shale material comprises of clay minerals hence, its anisotropy will be briefly 
described here.  
From the literature experiments on shale velocity, it can be concluded that its velocity anisotropy 
is dictated by intrinsic properties and it is a troublesome property in the seismic exploration field. 
Furthermore, anisotropy may cause a significant error in estimating dynamic Poisson’s ratio.  
As mentioned by Dewhurst and Siggins [9] many studies on shale anisotropy has been performed 
both in field and in the laboratory however, many of them did not have pore pressure control and 
were in an unsaturated state. This may lead to certain approximations and simplification of the 
problem and, as a result, most of those studies present shales as a transversely isotropic medium. 
Jones and Wang [20] have performed laboratory measurements of compressional and shear-wave 
velocities on Cretaceous shale samples from Williston basin, North Dakota. Experiments were 
performed as a function of confining pressure up to 4 kbar. It has been found from the study that 
shales behave as transversely isotropic elastic media. Compressional velocity has always been 
higher for propagation parallel to bedding than for propagation perpendicular to bedding. 
Furthermore, Jones and Wang results are in a good agreement with the Biot theory – compresional 
==PAGE:54==
49 
wave velocity has always been higher for saturated rock than for dry rock, and the reverse applies 
to shear wave velocity.  
Another experiment has been performed by Hornby [17] on Kimmeridge Clay shale from a North 
Sea borehole. Tests were performed in drained, saturated state as a function of confining pressure. 
It has been found that the examined shale was highly anisotropic: texturally and elastically. 
Anisotropy as high as 38 % for compressional wave (Thomsen’s parameter ε) and up to 58 % for 
shear wave (Thomsen’s parameter γ). The main reason for such a high values for Thomsen’s 
parameters was the change in particles alignment and decreasing porosity.  
However, anisotropy of shale cannot be simplified just to the dependence on intrinsic properties 
or versatility of stress field acting on a given material. Additional factors influencing velocity and 
anisotropy of shales are kerogen content (organic richness) as well as physiochemical interaction 
with pore fluids. Additionally, smectite content has to be considered as well due to the fact that it 
contains water in its mineral structure [17, 16].  
Dewhurst and Siggins [9] have performed an experiment on Muderong Shale from Carnarvon 
Basin on the Northwest Shalf of Australia. They have emphasized the influence of smectite on the 
anisotropy of shale as the material was composed of illite-smectite and quartz layers. Velocity 
measurements were performed in drained conditions with initial confining and pore pressures of 
10 MPa and 5 MPa respectively. This allowed for full saturation of shale at the beginning of the 
experiment contrary to other experiment described above and those found in the literature which 
were usually performed ‘dry’, unsaturated or partially saturated when drained to atmosphere. 
Dewhurst and Siggins [9] have found Muderong Shale to be highly anisotropic with Thomsen’s 
parameters ε and γ being 0.3 and 0.6 respectively, under low stress conditions. Those results are in 
a good agreement with other smectite-rich shale experiments (Thomsen [29], Hornby [17]) 
however, contrary to Hornby, authors’ state that stress relief microfractures account for significant 
increase in anisotropy. Furthermore, “the highest anisotropies in physical properties are usually 
expected in the finest-grained materials” [9] which is also a reason for high anisotropy of 
Muderong Shale.  
4.3.3. 
Influence of smectite on anisotropy 
Paleogene clay is a smectite rich material which has been presented in Table 1. It is a mineral used 
in civil engineering to impede water due to its water absorbing properties. It is composed of silicate 
layers in a form of plates with a gibbsite layers in between in an s-g-s sequence. According to Fitts 
and Brown [10] water can be found in between those layers and can reach up to ~25 wt. % of the 
mineral. As a result, smectite-rich materials, like Paleogene clay from the Ølst formation which 
has exceptionally high content of this mineral, possess higher moisture content, higher porosity 
and lower bulk density caused by increased amount of water [2]. Therefore, it can be expected that 
smectite can have meaningful influence on elastic properties of a material and response of P-wave 
==PAGE:55==
50 
and S-wave velocities on this mineral. It has been described by Dewhurst and Siggins [9] who 
investigated the influence of fabric, microcracks and stress field on shale anisotropy. Except from 
other factors influencing shale anisotropy they have found a correlation between “mixed layer of 
illite-smectite content and P-wave transit time (reciprocal of velocity) […] in approximately 
constant vertical effective stress of 25 – 30 MPa” which is presented in Figure 32. 
 
Figure 32. Influence of illite-smectite content on P-wave transit time (reciprocal of velocity) for 
Muderong Shale examined by D. N. Dewhurst and A. F. Siggins [9] 
This clearly shows an influence of trapped interlayer water within smectite structure which 
decreases bulk density and affects ultrasonic wave velocity measurements. Furthermore, Tina G. 
Fitts and Kevin M. Brown [10] have examined stress-induced smectite dehydration in the  
N. Barbados accretionary wedge. Drained loading tests have been conducted on pure 
montmorillonite samples and dehydration of the material was indicated by reduction in Cl 
concentration of expelled fluids. It is due to the fact that smectite was expected to release fresh 
water into pores of samples hence, decreasing salinity of pore water. Results of those tests are 
presented in Figure 33 [10]. 
They have found that effective load as low as 1.3 MPa can have an influence on dehydration of 
the examined material. Through XRD analysis it has been shown that such a low effective load is 
enough to decrease basal spacing of smectite minerals from 18.5 to 15.4 Å. This is a clear evidence 
for the reduction of interlayer bound water within the smectite structure.  
 
==PAGE:56==
51 
 
Figure 33. Influence of the effective stress on chlorinity of the expelled pore fluids of the 
examined smectite-rich sample [10] 
 
 
==PAGE:57==
52 
 
==PAGE:58==
53 
5. ANISOTROPY CALCULATION 
Basing on the theoretical background of elastic anisotropy it is known that there are three 
parameters determining anisotropy of a rock: ε, δ, and γ. It is a common knowledge that ε parameter 
is used as the one defining “the anisotropy” of a medium because it relates horizontal and vertical 
P-wave velocities. However, it has little importance when the near vertical anisotropy is 
considered. It is determined by the δ which incorporates the use of different elastic moduli which 
do not include the horizontal velocity parameter C11. Independence of ε on nearly-vertical 
anisotropy can be explained by the fact that in the equation for phase velocity, ε is multiplied by 
sin4θ which gives a value very close to 0. Therefore, it is stated that the δ determines near-vertical 
anisotropy as it has the biggest influence on phase velocity vp. Equations for phase velocities 
(pressure wave, shear horizontal wave, and shear vertical wave) in a weakly anisotropic medium 
are as follows: 
𝑣𝑃(𝜃) = 𝛼0(1 + 𝛿sin2 𝜃cos2 𝜃+ 𝜀sin4 𝜃) 
(34) 
𝑣𝑆𝑉(𝜃) = 𝛽0 [1 + 𝛼0
2
𝛽0
2 (𝜀−𝛿) sin2 𝜃cos2 𝜃] 
(35) 
𝑣𝑆𝐻(𝜃) = 𝛽0(1 + 𝛾sin2 𝜃) 
(36) 
As it has been mentioned before, arrival times for shear velocities could not be obtained from 
measurements with the desired accuracy as they would yield inaccurate and unreliable results. 
Hence, unfortunately δ and γ could not be calculated as they are controlled by S-waves velocity. 
The ε parameter is the only one that could be calculated which has been done in accordance with 
Equation 33a. Data for those calculations have been obtained from P-wave measurements for 
horizontal and vertical samples for 3 MPa pressure stage. Results of those calculations are 
presented in Table 9. 
As it can be seen from Table 9, ε do not exceed 0.2 which is the limit for weak anisotropy in 
accordance with Thomsen [29]. It is even negative for sample 1, 29, and 95 which is totally normal 
as similar results have been presented by Thomsen and some literature defines VTI media having 
ε as low as -0.375 [6]. Therefore, the obtained values of ε prove the material to be weakly 
anisotropic.  
Thomsen’s parameter ε has also been calculated for drained elastic modulus Mdrain. It has been 
done by replacing C11 and C33 parameters in Equation 33a with drained modulus for horizontal 
and vertical samples respectively. Results of those calculations are presented in Table 10.

As it can be seen from Table 10, there are substantial differences in ε values when compared with 
the ones from Table 9. It is due to the fact that values of Mdrain for vertical and horizontal samples 
differ much more than vp. Drained modulus includes deformation of a sample not only due to 
elastic change but also the one caused by creep. In reverse, saturated modulus calculated from  
P-wave velocity (Table 9) reflects elastic changes of a material only. A comparison of ε from both 
methods is presented in Figure 34. 
==PAGE:60==
55 
 
Figure 34. Comparison of Thomsen's parameters calculated from P-wave velocity (blue line) 
and Mdrain (red line) 
The obtained results clearly show some anisotropic properties of the Paleogene clay. Most likely 
it is caused by layered structure of the material as shown in Figure 27a. Furthermore, in the case 
of folded material (sample 31) the anisotropic properties can be explained by folding of the 
Tertiary sequence during glaciation. Therefore, it also possesses layered structure which may be 
positioned in different direction than in the case of intact samples. As a result, ε calculated from 
Mdrain takes negative value for sample 31. Similarly, sample 89 could have been folded during 
formation of a material however, it has not been stated in work done by Awadalkarim [2] whether 
the sample is intact or folded. As far as intact samples 2 and 93 are concerned, their ε from Mdrain 
is also slightly lower than zero, which may indicate slight deviation from vertical transverse 
isotropy (VTI). Unfortunately, due to the very poor quality of S-wave velocity measurements, it 
was not possible to calculate δ, and γ parameters which could tell a little bit more about type of 
anisotropy occurring in Paleogene clay.

presented in Table 11 and in Figure 35.

As it can be seen from Figure 35 there are significant differences of the anisotropic parameter 
depending on the formation. For both samples from Æbelø intact formation, anisotropy both 
decreases (sample 1) and increases (sample 2) with the increase in the applied load. Whereas, for 
Røsnæs intact anisotropy decreases with the increase in pressure for both samples (8 and 29). As 
far as Ølst formation is concerned, there is an increase in ε value for increasing load for folded 
sample 31 and reverse situation takes place for intact sample 93. The same happens for Lillebælt 
formation: one sample possesses increasing anisotropy for increasing load (sample 89) and the 
other one (sample 95) has decreasing anisotropy with the increase in axial load. Hogh calcite 
content of sample 95 might have an influence on its response to the applied load. Furthermore, 
there are some unexpected results in the case of sample 31. Value of ε parameter is decreasing in 
the range of 0.6 – 2 MPa and then it jumps to the highest value for 3 MPa. A similar situation 
happens for sample 93 and 89. There might be several reasons behind variations of anisotropy with 
the changing axial load and analysis of those results will be presented at the end of this section.

similar to those presented above. There is a visible difference in ε for different pressure stages of 
each sample. Once again, there are fluctuations in Thomsen’s parameter between 3 MPa and the 
rest of the stages for samples 31, 93 and 89 which yet has to be explained. What is interesting, 
contrary to ultrasonic anisotropy presented in Figure 35, drained anisotropy is decreasing for 
sample 2, 31 and 89 with the increase in axial load. Whereas, for sample 8 there is an increase in 
drained anisotropy with the decrease in ultrasonic anisotropy.

5.2. Analysis of anisotropy calculations 
As far as the results presented in Table 9 and 10 are concerned, it has been shown that Palaeogene 
clay from the Fehmarn Belt area is a weakly anisotropic material with a trend towards vertical 
transverse anisotropy. It is most likely caused by geological setting of the area and deposition of 
clay in a layered fashion. Furthermore, anisotropy might also be influenced by mineralogy of the 
material as Palaeogene clay is rich in smectite. As a mineral, smectite swells when in contact with 
water. As a result, there might have been a bound interlayer water within structure of clay. Hence, 
higher content of water, than normally, would decrease bulk density of the sample influencing  
P-wave velocity measurements. What is more, samples preparation might have had a significant 
influence on measurements. Some samples were difficult to cut therefore, there could be some 
deviations in samples size and water content which has been presented in Table 4. Once again, 
saturation could influence bulk density of the material.  
As far as the load dependent anisotropy is concerned, it could be partially influenced by smectite 
as well. Increase in axial load causes stress-induced loss of water contained by smectite especially 
that experiments were conducted in drained state. This could explain strange behavior of sample 
31, 93, and 89 which has a sudden increase/decrease in ε value above 2 MPa. Especially that some 
authors [10] have proven reduction of water within the mineral when a smectite-rich sample of  
a material has been subjected to load above 1.3 MPa.  
Although Palaeogene clay is a very soft material it might have microfractures influencing 
anisotropic properties of the material. It is certainly true when anisotropic stress conditions are 
applied to a sample as it has been done by Awadalkarim [2]. If the microfractures or microcracks 
are parallel to the principal stresses, they are in the best orientation for opening. This could be an 
explanation why strain for vertical samples is always higher than for horizontal samples  
==PAGE:64==
59 
(Figure 7 – 11), except for sample 89. However, layered structure of the material seems to be a 
more reasonable explanation. Furthermore, as it has been described by Heilman and Clausen et al. 
[16] clay that is present in Fehmarn Belt region, and in most of the regions of Denmark, is fine 
grained in most of the cases. According to Dewhurst and Siggins [9] grain size and grain 
refinement has a significant influence on anisotropic properties of the material and is the highest 
for the finest – grained materials. Thus, it might also influence anisotropic physical properties of 
the examined clay.  
 
 
==PAGE:65==
60 
 
==PAGE:66==
61 
6. DISCUSSION 
The research in this study was directed to finding the evidence of anisotropic properties of clay 
material from the Fehmarn Belt area. The investigation was based on triaxial and ultrasonic 
velocity measurements performed by A. Awadalkarim [2]. Ten core samples have been tested in 
horizontal, vertical, and tangential plane out of which eight were suitable for anisotropic 
calculations. It was due to the fact that quality of triaxial testing data for sample 15 were of poor 
quality which was the same case for sonic data of sample 37. 
As far as the triaxial tests are concerned, they were conducted under the axial load going from 0.6 
MPa up to 3 MPa which was followed by unloading down to 0.6 MPa of axial load. Confining 
pressure applied to samples was set in a way that allowed for a ratio of 0.55 between the radial 
load and axial load during four measuring stages: 0.6 MPa, 1 MPa, 2 MPa, and 3 MPa. When 
further analysis of tests have been performed a major error has been found. Increase in radial and 
axial load should be gradual so that the ratio σr/σa = 0.55 would be kept reasonably constant. 
However, data from those experiments have revealed that for pressure stages of 2 MPa and 3 MPa 
there was a gradual increase in radial pressure for 2 hours with almost no increase in axial load 
which jumped from one pressure stage to another within just a few seconds. This procedure could 
have caused undesired creep and cracks within the sample. Furthermore, a very rapid increase in 
axial load did not allow samples for full drainage especially that they were not kept at a given 
pressure sufficiently long. It has been found that in order to ensure almost full consolidation of a 
sample (>95 %), consolidation time should be equal to around 260 – 290 minutes depending on a 
clay sample and pressure stage. As this has not been achieved, it cannot be stated that velocity 
measurements were performed at fully drained state. This could have potentially influenced 
velocity measurements (increased amount of pore water within samples) as well as on deformation 
measurements (increased strain due to creep and rapid rise in axial load). However, as no other 
data were available, calculations were performed on this set of measurements and should be treated 
with appropriate caution.  
Furthermore, plots for S-waves depicted a huge ‘fluctuations’ in the received signal which did not 
allow to calculate arrival times. The possible explanation to this problem is that pressure applied 
to samples was not high enough for S-waves to cross the sample as there has not been a full contact 
between the sample and loading pistons. Furthermore, S-wave could have undergone refraction 
from clay minerals and interfered with P-wave which resulted in the noise. A possible solution has 
been presented – calculation of arrival times from Poisson’s ratio. However, accuracy of those 
calculations would be highly doubtful. This resulted in calculation of only one Thomsen’s 
parameter, ε. Additionally, anisotropic parameter ε has also been calculated for drained modulus 
in order to investigate whether any differences in those two calculation methods are present.  
==PAGE:67==
62 
 
 
==PAGE:68==
63 
7. CONCLUSIONS 
Laboratory tests and calculations in this study were performed in order to verify mechanical 
properties of clay samples from the Fehmarn Belt area. The main purpose of this study was to 
investigate if the material behaves as weakly anisotropic when loaded both axially and radially. 
Performed work have led to the following conclusions: 
 
 Stress – strain experiments have showed significant differences in the behavior of clay in 
horizontal, vertical, and 45ᴼ plane. Figures 7 – 11 have showed that mechanical properties of 
the considered samples are different. It is due to the fact that their response to the applied load 
resulted in different amount of deformation from the initial sample length. In all cases (except 
sample 89) vertical core plug showed higher strain than material from horizontal and tangential 
planes.  
 
 Velocity measurements used for calculation of saturated modulus, show difference in calculated 
elastic modulus when compared to drained elastic modulus. This shows the influence of pore 
fluid velocity of ultrasonic wave passing the sample. As a result there is a decrease in bulk 
modulus of the material. However, sonic wave elastic modulus is still higher than drained 
modulus which is caused by the fact that Mdrain is also influenced by plastic deformation of a 
sample which is not detected by Msat.  
 
 Gassmann’s equation have been employed to calculate dry modulus of the solid clay frame. 
According to Awadalkarim [2] the obtained Mdry is slightly higher than the Mdrain however, from 
Figures 23 – 25 with logarithmic scales it can be seen that difference is rather substantial. 
Probably, it is caused by the fact that dry modulus has been calculated from the saturated 
modulus Msat which do not account for plastic deformation but still, it can be used as a good 
estimate of elastic properties of a porous material frame. 
 
 The calculated moduli of elasticity, Mdrain and Msat have showed an anisotropic behavior of a 
material as the axial load on samples have been increased. Generally, increased load had a much 
higher influence on the change in drained modulus than on the change in saturated modulus. 
The possible explanation is the one that have already been stated – influence of plastic 
deformation on Mdrain. 
 
 Calculation of the anisotropic parameter ε led to the conclusion that clay from Fehmarn Belt 
area behaves as weakly anisotropic (ε<0.2). It can be explained by layered structure of the 
material caused by slow, gradual deposition, one layer after another. Whereas, in the case of 
==PAGE:69==
64 
folded clay (sample 31) variations in anisotropy can be caused by additional change in the 
direction of layering.  
 
 Thomsen’s parameter ε has also been calculated for drained modulus Mdrain which gave a more 
extreme results than in the case of Msat. Much higher values of ε have been obtained: from  
-0.079 up to 1.535 when compared to ε calculated from Msat, -0.060 up to 0.117. Once again 
the possible explanation to this difference is the influence of plastic deformation on Mdrain. 
 
 As far as the load dependent anisotropy is concerned, it have showed the most interesting 
results. As a fine-grained material, Fehmarn Belt clay is susceptible to anisotropic properties 
much more that coarser materials especially in the case of anisotropic loading which has been 
applied in experiments presented in this study. Increasing loading of samples causes decrease 
in anisotropy for some of them and increase for the rest. Decreasing anisotropy can be explained 
by the fact that the examined clay is rich in smectite. The mineral has swelling properties 
therefore, it contains water trapped between layers of the mineral. It is highly possible that with 
the increase in load, smectite expels water at a certain pressure. As a result strain of the sample 
and P-wave velocity might undergo significant changes. Strain would increase due to the 
decreasing space between minerals and bound water. Whereas, P-wave velocity would increase 
for the same reason – better contact between grains of the material and decreased amount of 
trapped interlayer water.  
 
 Changes in load dependent anisotropy might also be caused by micro-fractures and  
micro-cracks. If those cracks are parallel to the axial load, they would remain open. As a result, 
vertical cracks in horizontal sample would stay open whereas, vertical cracks in vertical sample 
would be closed by axial load which is higher than the applied confining pressure. This could 
explain why anisotropy is decreasing with load for most of the samples but is increasing for the 
rest of them (sample 29 and 89).  
 
 Load dependent anisotropy has also been calculated from drained modulus and it only confirms 
previous conclusions: plastic deformation has a huge importance when Mdrain and Msat are 
compared. As a result, range of anisotropy for Mdrain is much higher as well.  
 
 
==PAGE:70==
65 
8. RECOMMENDATIONS 
 Perform electron microscopy in order to define microstructure of clay samples. This could 
help to see if micro-cracks and minor fractures do exist in the material.  
 Establish better control of actuators in order to ensure proper control of the loading pistons 
which are responsible for axial load transferred onto samples. 
 Ensure that samples have undergone full consolidation ~100% so that there is no or very 
little influence of pore fluid on the results of Mdrain and Msat. 
 Further studies of the Gassmann equation are required due to the fact that results presented 
in this thesis are not in full agreement with conclusions stated by Awadalkarim [2]. It is 
probably caused by different depiction of data – linear scale has been used by Awadalkarim 
whereas, plots in this report are presented on logarithmic scale.

LIST OF SYMBOLS 
 
ρ – density [g/cm3] 
ρb – bulk density [g/cm3] 
ρd (ρdry)– dry density [g/cm3] 
ρdi– initial dry density [g/cm3] 
ρg – grain density [g/cm3] 
σ – stress [MPa] 
σpc’ – pre-consolidation stress [MPa] 
σvo’ – in-situ vertical effective stress 
Δσ – change in stress [MPa] 
ε – strain [%] 
Δε – change in strain [%] 
λ, μ – Lame’s parameters 
ø – porosity  
ε, γ, δ – Thomsen’s parameters of anisotropy 
α0 – ultrasonic horizontal P-wave velocity [m/s] 
β0 – ultrasonic vertical P-wave velocity [m/s] 
Å – unit of length, angstrom 
Θ – phase angle between the wavefront and the  
       vertical axis 
vsv – ultrasonic shear vertical velocity [m/s] 
vsh – ultrasonic shear horizontal velocity [m/s] 
cv – consolidation coefficient [m2/s] 
e – void ratio 
K0 – coefficient of Earth’s pressure at rest 
K0NC – normally consolidated K0 
OCR – Over Consolidation Ratio 
v – velocity [m/s] 
L – sample’s length [mm] 
tarrival – arrival time of the ultrasonic  
wavefront [s] 
tdelay – delay time of ultrasonic wave [s] 
tactual – arrival time minus the delay time [s] 
w – weight of a sample [g] 
V – volume of a sample [cm3] 
wi – water content [%] 
Wi – initial weight of a sample [g] 
Wdry – final weight of a sample after drying [g] 
T – time factor for drainage 
H – half the specimen height [m] 
t – time of consolidation [min] 
Mdrain – drained modulus [MPa] 
Mdry – dry modulus [MPa] 
Msat – saturated modulus [MPa] 
Mfluid – fluid modulus [MPa] 
Mgrain – grain modulus [MPa] 
vp – P-wave velocity [m/s] 
vs – S-wave velocity [m/s] 
υ – Poisson’s ratio 
E – Young’s modulus [MPa] 
G – shear modulus [MPa] 
Cijkl – elastic modulus tensor 
c33, c44, c11, c66, c13 – independent components  
 
 
          of elasticity
